The sting,  it is now used to deliver venom exclusively; there are actually a few wasps outside aculeata that have dual purpose ovipositors that can sting and lay eggs, however they are far less efficient at delivering venom and less painful than true aculeata stings. Having lost the ability to use the ovipositor as an egg-laying tube, stinging parasitoids like tarantula hawks and velvet ants lay eggs out of a different opening near the base of the sting onto their victims. \nMany members of aculeata however, have found an alternative use for their stings, defence. The sting allows the delivery of venom through the soft skin of vertebrates, causing pain in the woodbe attacker and hopefully driving it away. This potential for pain has caught the imagination of the public, most famously in something called the Schmidt insect sting pain index. This reaseach seams to indicate that social wasps have more painful stings to defend their brood. \nStings are pretty effective at deterring predators, and some organisms have taken notice and have developed adaptations to take advantage of stinging hymenoptera for their own defense. In the dry forests of Santa Rosa National Park in Northwestern Costa Rica, grows a spindly little acacia tree in the genus Vacellia. Its leaves and bark bustle with tiny ants of the genus Pseudomyrmex, which guard the plant which provides them with everything they need.\nOther organisms take advantage of the signals these insects use to warn potential predators about their painful stings. Many organisms can recognize the bold black and yellow stripes of a bee or wasp. This signal has been hijacked by certain flies. Some spiders also mimic ants. This dishonest warning is called Batesian mimicry.\nNo matter if you think the Schmidt sting pain index is a good bit of research or not, one thing for sure about the index is it an excellent piece of science communication, bringing the order hymenoptera to the attention of the public in an exciting way. These insects don\u2019t want to sting us, only putting themselves in danger to do so when they feel threatened themselves or feel like their brood are in immediate danger. Give them space and everyone will be just fine. Warm Shower or Bath\n\nTaking a warm shower or bath 1-2 hrs before bed can improve sleep quality. Mediterranean Diet(MD)\n\nMD has many benefits for our body including our brain health as well as preventing the risk of dementia. Sleep Plans\n\nHaving a strict sleep plans and routines will surely break you out of insomnia and improve your overall sleep quality. I should start by saying that I\u2019m not a winter person.  I like spring and summer.  I like sunshine, warm weather and the beach. My natural inclination, therefore, would be to hibernate in the winter months, avoiding the great outdoors, avoiding social activities that take me outside the house, and staying warm and cosy inside.\r\n\r\n Regardless of the weather, however, research(1) has linked time spent outside, particularly in nature, with improved mood and emotional well-being, so it\u2019s worth wrapping up warm and getting outside whenever we can.  I\u2019ll admit, I don\u2019t take my own advice on this often enough, but yesterday, I decided to go for it. \r\n\r\n I have been exercising more frequently and regularly lately, since losing a lot of weight with the help of weight loss surgery - the exercise helps with the weight loss AND the weight loss makes exercise more enjoyable - but have managed to hurt both my shoulders in the process, so I\u2019ve had to back off some of my gym activity and exercise classes.  This meant I had to think about other ways to exercise that wouldn\u2019t strain my shoulders and I thought \u201cI know! In this particular study, increased light was only one of the factors; the other elements included in the research were regular brisk walks and daily vitamin and mineral supplements, with the aim of increasing the activity of neurotransmitters such as serotonin to achieve the improvements I just mentioned.  The researchers suggested some simple ways to increase our regular exposure to light, for example using a transparent umbrella when it rains, or choosing a window seat on a train or bus, or making sure you open the curtains or blinds on your windows, even if it\u2019s a dull day you\u2019d rather not look at.\r\n\r\n So, how can you build more time outside, or more daylight, into your day? Can we take some lesson activities outside?\r\n\r\n I hope this episode has helped you reflect on simple ways to look after your own emotional wellbeing and build on the amazing work you\u2019re already doing to support children\u2019s wellbeing. 10.1016/j.cub.2013.08.038\nhttp://www.cell.com/current-biology/retrieve/pii/S0960982213010440\n\nHighlights\n-The intertidal crustacean Eurydice pulchra exhibits circadian and tidal phenotypes\n-We have cloned and characterized the canonical circadian factors of Eurydice\n-While sensitive to CK1 inhibition, circadian and tidal clocks can be dissociated\n-Eurydice has a dedicated circatidal clock independent of its circadian clock\n\nSummary\nBackground: Tidal (12.4 hr) cycles of behavior and physiology adapt intertidal organisms to temporally complex coastal environments, yet their underlying mechanism is unknown. However, the very existence of an independent \"circatidal\" clock has been disputed, and it has been argued that tidal rhythms arise as a submultiple of a circadian clock, operating in dual oscillators whose outputs are held in antiphase i.e., ~12.4 hr apart. Results: We demonstrate that the intertidal crustacean Eurydice pulchra (Leach) exhibits robust tidal cycles of swimming in parallel to circadian (24 hr) rhythms in behavioral, physiological and molecular phenotypes. Importantly, ~12.4 hr cycles of swimming are sustained in constant conditions, they can be entrained by suitable stimuli, and they are temperature compensated, thereby meeting the three criteria that define a biological clock. Unexpectedly, tidal rhythms (like circadian rhythms) are sensitive to pharmacological inhibition of Casein kinase 1, suggesting the possibility of shared clock substrates. However, cloning the canonical circadian genes of E. pulchra to provide molecular markers of circadian timing and also reagents to disrupt it by RNAi revealed that environmental and molecular manipulations that confound circadian timing do not affect tidal timing. Thus, competent circadian timing is neither an inevitable nor necessary element of tidal timekeeping. Conclusions: We demonstrate that tidal rhythms are driven by a dedicated circatidal pacemaker that is distinct from the circadian system of E. pulchra, thereby resolving a long-standing debate regarding the nature of the circatidal mechanism.\n\nb) Circadian and Circalunar Clock Interactions in a Marine Annelid\nCell Reports, 26 September 2013, DOI: 10.1016/j.celrep.2013.08.031\nhttp://www.cell.com/cell-reports/retrieve/pii/S2211124713004725\n\nHighlights\n- Marine worms coexpress circadian clock gene orthologs in the forebrain and eye\n- The circalunar clock is functional in the absence of circadian clock oscillations\n- The circalunar clock changes the period length of circadian-clock-controlled behavior\n- The circalunar clock does not change the period of circadian molecular oscillations\n\nSummary\nLife is controlled by multiple rhythms. Although the interaction of the daily (circadian) clock with environmental stimuli, such as light, is well documented, its relationship to endogenous clocks with other periods is little understood. We establish that the marine worm Platynereis dumerilii possesses endogenous circadian and circalunar (monthly) clocks and characterize their interactions. The RNAs of likely core circadian oscillator genes localize to a distinct nucleus of the worm's forebrain. The worm's forebrain also harbors a circalunar clock entrained by nocturnal light. This monthly clock regulates maturation and persists even when circadian clock oscillations are disrupted by the inhibition of casein kinase 1d/e. Both circadian and circalunar clocks converge on the regulation of transcript levels. Furthermore, the circalunar clock changes the period and power of circadian behavior, although the period length of the daily transcriptional oscillations remains unaltered. They can be characterized through some particular elements called minutiae. The identification of a given fingerprint requires the matching of its minutiae against the minutiae of other fingerprints. Hence, fingerprint matching is a key process. The efficiency of current matching algorithms does not allow their use in large fingerprint databases; to apply them, a breakthrough in running performance is necessary. Nowadays, the minutia cylinder-code (MCC) is the best performing algorithm in terms of accuracy. However, a weak point of this algorithm is its computational requirements. In this paper, we present a GPU fingerprint matching system based on MCC. The many-core computing framework provided by CUDA on NVIDIA Tesla and GeForce hardware platforms offers an opportunity to enhance fingerprint matching. Through a thorough and careful data structure, computation and memory transfer design, we have developed a system that keeps its accuracy and reaches a speed-up up to 100.8\u00d7 compared with a reference sequential CPU implementation. A rigorous empirical study over captured and synthetic fingerprint databases shows the efficiency of our proposal. These results open up a whole new field of possibilities for reliable real time fingerprint identification in large databases.\nPublished in:\nInformation Forensics and Security, IEEE Transactions on Neuron. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.neuron.2013.09.031\n\u4f5c\u8005\uff1aAyden \uff08brainnews\u5275\u4f5c\u5718\u968a\u6210\u54e1\uff09\n\u6821\u5be9/\u6392\u7248\uff1aSimon \uff08brainnews\u7de8\u8f2f\u90e8\uff09\n1554981982", "title": "\u8fd110\u842c\u4eba\u7684\u6578\u64da\u5206\u6790\uff1a\u300c\u8607\u5927\u5f37\u6240\u60a3\u7684\u75c5\u300d\u98a8\u96aa\u57fa\u56e0\u4f4d\u9ede\u6709\u4e86\u65b0\u767c\u73fe", "channelId": "UCyTm_yvUdBEmHBlvNYNnSGQ"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT3M57S"}}], "idx_paper": "10.1016/j.ins.2013.09.031", "q": "10.1016/j.ins.2013.09.031"}, {"items": [{"statistics": {"dislikeCount": "1", "commentCount": "0", "likeCount": "1", "favoriteCount": "0", "viewCount": "205"}, "id": "KgnpX9dd_WM", "snippet": {"channelTitle": "FamilyDogProject", "tags": ["dog", "canine", "science", "research", "Department of Ethology", "cognition", "wolf", "wolves", "puppy", "gps", "vizsla", "dog group", "personality", "dominance"], "publishedAt": "2018-04-10T15:56:52Z", "description": "How does a group of family dogs decide the direction of their collective movements? Is there a leader, or is decision-making based on an egalitarian system? Is leadership related to social dominance status? We collected GPS trajectory data from an owner and her six dogs during several walks. We found that dogs adjusted their trajectories to that of the owner, that they periodically run away, then turn back and return to her in a loop. Tracks have unique features characterising individual dogs. Leading roles among the dogs are frequently interchanged, but leadership is consistent on a long timescale. Decisions about running away and turning back to the owner are not based on an egalitarian system; instead, leader dogs exert a disproportionate influence on the movement of the group. Leadership during walks is related to the dominance rank assessed in everyday agonistic situations; thus, the collective motion of a dog group is influenced by the underlying hierarchical social network. Leader/dominant dogs have a unique personality: they are more trainable, controllable, and aggressive, additionally they are older than follower/subordinate dogs. Dogs are an ideal model for understanding human social behaviour. Therefore, we address the possibility of conducting similar studies in humans, e.g. walking with children and detecting interactions between individuals.\nPaper: http://journals.plos.org/ploscompbiol/article?id=10.1371/journal.pcbi.1003446", "title": "Leadership are linked to dominance order and individual traits in dogs", "channelId": "UCbOc2RASuQAwrM7R1cqI4Uw"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT1M39S"}}], "idx_paper": "10.1371/journal.pcbi.1003446", "q": "10.1371/journal.pcbi.1003446"}, {"items": [{"statistics": {"dislikeCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "likeCount": "1", "favoriteCount": "0", "viewCount": "94"}, "id": "x_I_x_kg5Zw", "snippet": {"channelTitle": "Stefano Di Criscio", "tags": ["Dog", "Follow", "Leader", "Leadership", "Dominance", "Dogs", "Animals"], "publishedAt": "2014-01-27T00:09:51Z", "description": "Animation showing a 3 minute long part of a walk by the owner (black triangle) and her dogs (coloured circles), recorded with GPS devices. In the bottom right corner the real time is shown, the video is played at 5 times the real speed. The inset in the top right corner illustrates the total path of the owner during the walk which started at the origin. The small rectangle shows the area presented on the main plot. On the main plot, for each individual the thick, normal and thin lines show the trajectories of the last 2 s, 5 s and 20 s, respectively. The momentary leader-follower relationships found by the time windowed directional correlation delay method are shown with the kite-shaped highlighting: between the smaller equal-length sides (close to the right angle vertex) is the leader, while the acute angle vertex points towards the follower.\n\n- Dogs Follow the Leader\nhttp://news.sciencemag.org/biology/2014/01/scienceshot-dogs-follow-leader \n\nReference\nLeadership and Path Characteristics during Walks Are Linked to Dominance Order and Individual Traits in Dogs\nPLoS Comput Biol 10(1): e1003446. doi:10.1371/journal.pcbi.1003446\nhttp://www.ploscompbiol.org/article/info:doi/10.1371/journal.pcbi.1003446\n\nAbstract\nMovement interactions and the underlying social structure in groups have relevance across many social-living species. Collective motion of groups could be based on an \"egalitarian\" decision system, but in practice it is often influenced by underlying social network structures and by individual characteristics. We investigated whether dominance rank and personality traits are linked to leader and follower roles during joint motion of family dogs. We obtained high-resolution spatio-temporal GPS trajectory data (823,148 data points) from six dogs belonging to the same household and their owner during 14 30--40 min unleashed walks. We identified several features of the dogs' paths (e.g., running speed or distance from the owner) which are characteristic of a given dog. A directional correlation analysis quantifies interactions between pairs of dogs that run loops jointly. We found that dogs play the role of the leader about 50--85% of the time, i.e. the leader and follower roles in a given pair are dynamically interchangable. However, on a longer timescale tendencies to lead differ consistently. The network constructed from these loose leader--follower relations is hierarchical, and the dogs' positions in the network correlates with the age, dominance rank, trainability, controllability, and aggression measures derived from personality questionnaires. We demonstrated the possibility of determining dominance rank and personality traits of an individual based only on its logged movement data. The collective motion of dogs is influenced by underlying social network structures and by characteristics such as personality differences. Our findings could pave the way for automated animal personality and human social interaction measurements.", "title": "Dogs Follow the Leader", "channelId": "UCXGt8Ymi0WHsU-NogCuT9bg"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT34S"}}], "idx_paper": "10.1371/journal.pcbi.1003446", "q": "10.1371/journal.pcbi.1003446"}, {"items": [{"statistics": {"dislikeCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "likeCount": "0", "favoriteCount": "0", "viewCount": "22"}, "id": "N3zUxHvccC4", "snippet": {"channelTitle": "Vincent Bo\u017einov", "title": "Tinnitus Project", "publishedAt": "2020-04-10T01:52:49Z", "description": "Article 1.\nPiccirillo, J. We can place these implants following the technique described by Br\u00e5nemark, where a window in the upper portion of the sinus guides the zygomatic perforation and a part of the implant is exposed inside the sinus. We can modify this technique by raising and preserving the intra-maxillary mucosa that will retract over the implant or else, place them completely outside the maxillary sinus. In those patients submitted to this surgery to settle one dental prosthesis, it is essential to consider the nasal cavity and the paranasal sinuses conditions in the choice of surgical technique to be used. Functional or anatomical abnormalities such as chronic nasal obstruction, septal deviation or a maxilar ostium block needed to be correct previously, otherwise we will face a chronic infectious process that is difficult to solve. In this patient an evident septal deviation blocking the left middle meatus associated with chronic nasal obstruction was not considered. Some time after the procedure, recurrent crises of acute bacterial sinusitis began, treated several times without solution. After three years suffering from the sensation of weight on the face, daily nasal discharge, objective cacosmia and recurrent use of antibiotics, she underwent intranasal maxillary antrostomy associated with ethmoidectomy and septoplasty. A satisfactory symptomatic improvement was obtained, however, in the long term, the implants will remain at risk of extrusion.\nThere are no controlled and randomized studies that demonstrate better results of the zygomatic implant technique compared to bone grafting techniques for the treatment of atrophic maxillae. Thus, although some studies report that these implants have a success rate varying from 92 to 98%, with few surgical complications - orbital perforation being the most common of them, it is very important to select the candidates for surgery.\nReferences\nEsposito M, Worthington HV. The Healthpraxis", "publishedAt": "2020-05-29T10:59:30Z", "defaultAudioLanguage": "en", "description": "New Study Shows How Forward Head Posture Affects the Brain. \n\nhttps://spinalresearch.com.au/new-study-shows-how-forward-head-posture-affects-the-brain/ \n\nThanks to Clare McIvor at  February 11, 2020\n\n\u201cIn a neutral head position (with the lateral posture line running straight through the earlobes and shoulders, with shoulder blades retracted), the average human head weighs 10-12 pounds (4.5-5.5 Kilograms). However, as the head moves forward, this number increases drastically.\n\nAt 15 degrees, the head weighs 27 pounds (12.3 kilograms)\nAt 30 degrees, it increases to 40 pounds (18.2 kilograms)\nAt 45 degrees, it weighs 49 pounds (22.3 kilograms)\nAt 60 degrees, it exerts a force of 60 pounds on the cervical spine. (27.3 kilograms)\nAt 90 degrees, the force could not be measured.\n\nREFERENCES:\n\nIbrahim M Moustafa, Ahmed Youssef, Amal Ahbouch, May Tamim, Deed E. Feb 2018, pp. 81-91", "title": "Forward Head Posture!", "channelId": "UCrTsVhO4Z5nHa5hfFPHFyQQ"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT12M11S"}}], "idx_paper": "10.1016/j.media.2013.08.003", "q": "10.1016/j.media.2013.08.003"}, {"items": [{"statistics": {"dislikeCount": "0", "likeCount": "7", "favoriteCount": "0", "viewCount": "1369"}, "id": "gTj1yYa-Lnw", "snippet": {"channelTitle": "IJRR Multimedia", "title": "Quasi-static Manipulation of a Kirchhoff Elastic Rod Based on a Geometric Analysis...", "publishedAt": "2014-02-21T18:30:21Z", "description": "\"Quasi-static Manipulation of a Kirchhoff Elastic Rod Based on a Geometric Analysis of Equilibrium Configurations\"\nby Timothy Bretl and Zoe McCarthy\n\nInternational Journal of Robotics Research 33(1): 48-68\nhttp://dx.doi.org/10.1177/0278364912473169\n\nConsider a thin, flexible wire of fixed length that is held at each end by a robotic gripper. Any curve traced by this wire when in static equilibrium is a local solution to a geometric optimal control problem, with boundary conditions that vary with the position and orientation of each gripper. We prove that the set of all local solutions to this problem over all possible boundary conditions is a smooth manifold of finite dimension that can be parameterized by a single chart. We show that this chart makes it easy to implement a sampling-based algorithm for quasi-static manipulation planning. We characterize the performance of such an algorithm with experiments in simulation.", "channelId": "UC937VH2K4sQgijIkuI3YEFw"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT3M17S"}}], "idx_paper": "10.1177/0278364912473169", "q": "10.1177/0278364912473169"}, {"items": [{"statistics": {"dislikeCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "likeCount": "0", "favoriteCount": "0", "viewCount": "96"}, "id": "J1t7RCg1EqM", "snippet": {"channelTitle": "Aaya Shatila", "title": "Neuro3L | What is Pain? (UCSD BIPN 152- Spring 2019)", "publishedAt": "2019-05-28T19:55:34Z", "description": "What is Pain?\nScript:\n\n5 year old - Tzu-Hsuan Chen:\nHave you ever wondered why getting something like a cut hurts? When you get a cut, little messengers in your hand called neurons, that are all over your body, send a message to the brain, telling it that your hand was cut. Your brain receives the message, and now knows where the cut is and how much it hurts, ow! This feeling is what pain is, and might make you cry.\n\n13 year old - Aaya Shatila:\nWhen your body gets injured, your neurons send millions of messages to your brain about what\u2019s going on. Neurons consist of dendrites, cell bodies, and axons. The message that is now an electrical signal is received by the dendrites, added up by the cell body, and propagated down the neuron by the axon, eventually making it to the spine. The first neuron sends that electrical signal to another neuron through messenger molecules called neurotransmitters. The second neuron sends the signal to the brain. The brain further processes that signal in a special location, allowing us to sense pain.\n\nCollege - Cameron Haghshenas:\nWhen you receive noxious stimuli from an external source your nociceptors, specialized neurons, are stimulated. Depending on the stimulus, there are different kinds of nociceptors such as mechanical, thermal, or chemical that respond to pain, heat, and certain molecules respectively.  The now electrical signal, if the graded potential passes a certain threshold and becomes an action potential is then propagated down the peripheral nerve, eventually synapsing with a dorsal root ganglion/secondary neuron in the spinal cord of the central nervous system. Neurotransmitter Substance P is released from the primary, pre-synaptic neuron into the CNS where the secondary, post-synaptic neuron can then take it up via its dendrites. That secondary neuron decussates across the spinal cord via the spinothalamic tract and synapses in the thalamus. The thalamus, acting as the relay station of the brain, sends the appropriate signal via a third order neuron to the appropriate place in the somatosensory cortex. This is what allows us to perceive the painful sensation and pinpoint kinetically where it is occurring.\n\nSources:\nhttps://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3438523/\n\nhttps://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/books/NBK10967/\n\nhttps://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2964977/\n\nhttps://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/books/NBK21535/\n\nhttps://journals.plos.org/ploscompbiol/article?id=10.1371/journal.pcbi.1003439\n\nhttps://academic.oup.com/painmedicine/article/15/10/1669/1939615\n\nhttps://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4228136/\n\nhttps://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/books/NBK507824/\n\nhttp://www.jimmunol.org/content/199/5/1543\n\nhttps://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4904790/\n\nBIPN Multimedia", "title": "A Direct Method for Trajectory Optimization of Rigid Bodies Through Contact", "publishedAt": "2014-02-21T18:30:33Z", "description": "\"A Direct Method for Trajectory Optimization of Rigid Bodies Through Contact\"\nby Michael Posa, Cecilia Cantu, and Russ Tedrake\n\nInternational Journal of Robotics Research 33(1): 69-81\nhttp://dx.doi.org/10.1177/0278364913506757\n\nDirect methods for trajectory optimization are widely used for planning locally optimal trajectories of robotic systems. Many critical tasks, such as locomotion and manipulation, often involve impacting the ground or objects in the environment. Most state-of-the-art techniques treat the discontinuous dynamics that result from impacts as discrete modes and restrict the search for a complete path to a specified sequence through these modes. Here we present a novel method for trajectory planning of rigid-body systems that contact their environment through inelastic impacts and Coulomb friction. This method eliminates the requirement for a priori mode ordering. Motivated by the formulation of multi-contact dynamics as a Linear Complementarity Problem for forward simulation, the proposed algorithm poses the optimization problem as a Mathematical Program with Complementarity Constraints. We leverage Sequential Quadratic Programming to naturally resolve contact constraint forces while simultaneously optimizing a trajectory that satisfies the complementarity constraints. The method scales well to high-dimensional systems with large numbers of possible modes. We demonstrate the approach on four increasingly complex systems: rotating a pinned object with a finger, simple grasping and manipulation, planar walking with the Spring Flamingo robot, and high-speed bipedal running on the FastRunner platform.", "channelId": "UC937VH2K4sQgijIkuI3YEFw"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT57S"}}], "idx_paper": "10.1177/0278364913506757", "q": "10.1177/0278364913506757"}, {"items": [{"statistics": {"dislikeCount": "0", "commentCount": "2", "likeCount": "7", "favoriteCount": "0", "viewCount": "155"}, "id": "q2j53m65DKU", "snippet": {"channelTitle": "Charles Dawson", "publishedAt": "2020-05-12T18:08:31Z", "defaultAudioLanguage": "en-US", "description": "In this video, we describe our approach to using non-convex optimization to allow a robot to flip a pancake. However, the very existence of an independent \"circatidal\" clock has been disputed, and it has been argued that tidal rhythms arise as a submultiple of a circadian clock, operating in dual oscillators whose outputs are held in antiphase i.e., ~12.4 hr apart. Results: We demonstrate that the intertidal crustacean Eurydice pulchra (Leach) exhibits robust tidal cycles of swimming in parallel to circadian (24 hr) rhythms in behavioral, physiological and molecular phenotypes. Importantly, ~12.4 hr cycles of swimming are sustained in constant conditions, they can be entrained by suitable stimuli, and they are temperature compensated, thereby meeting the three criteria that define a biological clock. Unexpectedly, tidal rhythms (like circadian rhythms) are sensitive to pharmacological inhibition of Casein kinase 1, suggesting the possibility of shared clock substrates. However, cloning the canonical circadian genes of E. pulchra to provide molecular markers of circadian timing and also reagents to disrupt it by RNAi revealed that environmental and molecular manipulations that confound circadian timing do not affect tidal timing. Thus, competent circadian timing is neither an inevitable nor necessary element of tidal timekeeping. Conclusions: We demonstrate that tidal rhythms are driven by a dedicated circatidal pacemaker that is distinct from the circadian system of E. pulchra, thereby resolving a long-standing debate regarding the nature of the circatidal mechanism.\n\nb) Circadian and Circalunar Clock Interactions in a Marine Annelid\nCell Reports, 26 September 2013, DOI: 10.1016/j.celrep.2013.08.031\nhttp://www.cell.com/cell-reports/retrieve/pii/S2211124713004725\n\nHighlights\n- Marine worms coexpress circadian clock gene orthologs in the forebrain and eye\n- The circalunar clock is functional in the absence of circadian clock oscillations\n- The circalunar clock changes the period length of circadian-clock-controlled behavior\n- The circalunar clock does not change the period of circadian molecular oscillations\n\nSummary\nLife is controlled by multiple rhythms. Although the interaction of the daily (circadian) clock with environmental stimuli, such as light, is well documented, its relationship to endogenous clocks with other periods is little understood. We establish that the marine worm Platynereis dumerilii possesses endogenous circadian and circalunar (monthly) clocks and characterize their interactions. The RNAs of likely core circadian oscillator genes localize to a distinct nucleus of the worm's forebrain. The worm's forebrain also harbors a circalunar clock entrained by nocturnal light. This monthly clock regulates maturation and persists even when circadian clock oscillations are disrupted by the inhibition of casein kinase 1d/e. Both circadian and circalunar clocks converge on the regulation of transcript levels. Furthermore, the circalunar clock changes the period and power of circadian behavior, although the period length of the daily transcriptional oscillations remains unaltered. "2014-01-08T09:33:47Z", "description": "Presentation of the Ergo-Robot Experiment by Pierre-Yves Oudeyer, during  \"Symposium on Language Acquisition and Language Evolution\", Stockholm University, Royal Academy of Sciences, Stockholm, Sweden (organised by Francesco Lacerda and Bjorn Lindblom). \n\n=== Summary\n\nIn a big egg that has just opened, a tribe of young robotic creatures evolves and explores its environment, wreathed by a large zero that symbolizes the \"origin.\" Beyond their innate capabilities, they are outfitted with mechanisms that allow them to learn new skills and invent their own language. Endowed with artificial curiosity, they explore objects around them, as well as the effect their vocalizations produce on humans. Human, also curious to see what these creatures can do, react with their own gestures, creating a loop of interaction which progressively self-organizes into a new communication system established between man and ergo-robots.\n\nThe Ergo-Robot Experiment addresses a very important methodological and experimental need in Developmental Robotics: evaluating how algorithmic architectures for learning and development can scale up to long-term real world robot experiments (i.e. robot operating and learning continuously for several weeks or months). For this goal, which Ergo-Robots addresses [P28], and in the context of a research lab, one needs an experimental platform which is very robust, with precise and reliable motor control, easy to use and maintain, relatively cheap, and reconfigurable (in order to evaluate algorithms on robots with different morphologies, i.e. different robots).\n\nThe experiment was presented at the exhibition \"Mathematics: A Beautiful Elsewhere\" at Fondation Cartier pour l'Art Contemporain (scenography made in collaboration with film director David Lynch) provided in itself a large impact for scientific dissemination of our research activities towards the general public, being explained by student scientific mediators to around 70000 visitors (trained students were continuously present in the exhibition), and being reported multiple times in the general press (e.g. France 2, France Inter, France Culture, RFI, BFM TV, Slate.fr, Sciences et Avenir, New Scientist, Financial Times). \n\nts software and hardware development was realized by the INRIA ENSTA ParisTech Flowers team in collaboration with University of Bordeaux/Labri: J\u00e9rome B\u00e9chu, Fabien B\u00e9nureau, Haylee Fogg, Paul Fudal, Hugo Gimbert, Matthieu Lapeyre, Olivier Ly, Olivier Mangin, Pierre-Yves Oudeyer, Pierre Rouanet. Scenography was realized in collaboration with David Lynch and his team. The shares can appear as noise-like pixels or as meaningful images; but it will arouse suspicion and increase interception risk during transmission of the shares. Hence, VSS schemes suffer from a transmission risk problem for the secret itself and for the participants who are involved in the VSS scheme. To address this problem, we proposed a natural-image-based VSS scheme (NVSS scheme) that shares secret images via various carrier media to protect the secret and the participants during the transmission phase. The proposed (n,n)- NVSS scheme can share one digital secret image over n-1 arbitrary selected natural images (called natural shares) and one noise-like share. The natural shares can be photos or hand-painted pictures in digital form or in printed form. The noise-like share is generated based on these natural shares and the secret image. The unaltered natural shares are diverse and innocuous, thus greatly reducing the transmission risk problem. We also propose possible ways to hide the noise-like share to reduce the transmission risk problem for the share. Experimental results indicate that the proposed approach is an excellent solution for solving the transmission risk problem for the VSS schemes.\nPublished in:\nInformation Forensics and Security, IEEE Transactions on  (Volume:9 ,  Issue: 1 )\nDate of Publication:\nJan. 2014\n\nPage(s):\n    88 - 98 \nISSN :\n    1556-6013 \nINSPEC Accession Number:\n    13973146 \nDOI:\n    10.1109/TIFS.2013.2292509 \n\nDate of Publication :\n    25 November 2013 \nDate of Current Version :\n    17 December 2013 \nIssue Date :\n    Jan. 2014 \nSponsored by :\n    IEEE Signal Processing Society \nPublisher:\n    IEEE \n\n\nMob: 9985613074, 7396708351\n\nSite: www.newieeeprojects.com\n\nEmail: newieeeprojects@gmail.com", "title": "Digital Image Sharing by Diverse Image Media   | IEEE | IEEE projects 2014", "channelId": "UC1bqqQAlQgOUm4aN4n3mA4w"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT33S"}}], "idx_paper": "10.1109/TIFS.2013.2292509", "q": "10.1109/TIFS.2013.2292509"}, {"items": [{"statistics": {"dislikeCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "likeCount": "4", "favoriteCount": "0", "viewCount": "926"}, "id": "X51w19TVGGQ", "snippet": {"channelTitle": "Zhangping Wei", "tags": ["Solitary wave", "wave structure interaction", "wave breaking"], "publishedAt": "2013-10-10T03:03:24Z", "description": "A physical experiment reported by Swigler (2009) was simulated by using a depth-integrated non-hydrostatic phase-resolving wave model, CCHE2D-NHWAVE (Wei & Jia, 2013,2014). This video explores the basics of lightning and gives an introduction to Terrestrial Gamma-ray Flashes (TGFs), which are bursts of gamma rays within thunderclouds.\n\nTGFs were first discovered by the Compton Gamma Ray Observatory (CGRO), bringing to light a new natural phenomena that forms gamma rays, other than radioactive decay and gamma ray bursts in space. In fact, before this discovery, scientists thought that gamma ray radiation of this power could only form near the sun, in black holes, large galaxies, or neutron stars. Thus, understanding the mechanisms of TGFs could take us a step closer in studying the processes that may occur elsewhere in space and potentially preventing people from being exposed to its intense radiation. \n 2020 Breakthrough Junior Challenge\n#breakthroughjuniorchallenge #physics #particlephysics #thunderstorms #lightning #tgfs\n \nCITATIONS\nVideos\n[The Element].
